<t0>First chapter</t0>
<t1>The studio was filled with the languid scent of roses; when a light summer breeze wandered among the trees in the garden, the heavy fragrance of lilacs or the more delicate perfume of pink-flowered thorn trees wafted in through the open door.</t1>
<t2>Lord Henry Wotton, who was lying in the corner of the divan adorned with Iranian saddlebags, smoking countless cigarettes as was his habit, could make out the honey-colored, honey-sweet flowers of the yellow wisteria tree from where he was.</t2> <t3>The tree's trembling branches seemed to struggle under the burden of such flame-like beauty.</t3> <t4>Occasionally, the shadows of birds, like dreams, would fall upon the long, silken curtains stretched in front of the large window.</t4> <t5>At first glance, these curtains, which evoked a Japanese atmosphere, reminded Lord Henry of the pale, emerald-faced painters of Tokyo; painters who tried to convey movement and speed through the necessarily static medium of art.</t5> <t6>The dull hum of bees, either trying to make their way through the tall, uncut grass or circling monotonously around the dusty, gilded horns of the sprawling French marigolds, seemed to make the tranquility of the surroundings all the more tedious.</t6> <t7>The faint hum of London resembled the bass notes of a distant organ melody.</t7>
<t8>In the very center of the room, on a standing canvas, stood a portrait of an extraordinarily beautiful young man.</t8> <t9>In front of it, a little further away, sat the painter of the portrait: Basil Hallward, who had caused quite a stir a few years ago by suddenly disappearing and giving rise to a host of strange speculations.</t9>
<t10>As he gazed at the elegant and handsome figure reflected in the artist's work, a smile of pleasure spread across his face, and it seemed as if he wanted to linger there for a while.</t10> <t11>However, he suddenly started and straightened up, closing his eyes and pressing his fingertips against his eyelids as if trying to trap a strange dream in his mind that he feared losing upon waking.</t11>
<t12>Lord Henry said in a lazy voice, "This is your best work, Basil, the finest thing you have done."</t12> <t13>"You must send it to the Grosvenor Gallery next year, without a doubt.</t13> <t14>The Academy is too large, too vulgar.</t14> <t15>Whenever I went there, I either couldn't see the paintings because of the crowd, which is a terrible thing, or I couldn't see the people because of the multitude of paintings, which is even worse.</t15> <t16>Believe me, the only option is the Grosvenor Gallery.</t16><t17>"</t17>
<t18>The painter replied, "I don't think I'll send this anywhere," throwing his head back with that strange motion that had always amused his friends when he was in Oxford.</t18> <t19>"No, I won't send it anywhere.</t19><t20>"</t20>
<t21>Lord Henry raised his eyebrows; he looked at his friend in astonishment through the thin, blue rings of smoke spiraling up from the thick, opium-laced cigarette he was smoking.</t21>
<t22>“Aren't you going to send it anywhere?</t22> <t23>My dear friend, why?</t23> <t24>Do you have a reason?</t24> <t25>You painters are such strange people!</t25> <t26>You do everything to gain fame.</t26> <t27>And as soon as you gain it, you want to squander it!</t27> <t28>What you're doing is nonsense, because if there's anything worse in this world than being talked about, it's not being talked about.</t28> <t29>Such a portrait would place you higher than all the young painters in England and make the old ones die of envy; though I don't know if the old ones have any feelings left.</t29><t30>”</t30>
<t31>Painter Hallward said, "I know you will mock me, but still, I really can't exhibit this painting."</t31> <t32>"I have put so much of myself into it.</t32><t33>"</t33>
<t34>Lord Henry lay full length on the divan and laughed.</t34>
<t35>"Yes, I knew you would laugh, but I'm still telling the truth, no matter what you say."</t35><t36>"</t36>
<t37>“You added a lot of yourself, huh!</t37> <t38>God bless you, Basil, I never knew you were so arrogant.</t38> <t39>I can't see any resemblance between your coal-black hair, your harsh-lined, swarthy face and this young Adonis; believe me, this young man looks like he's made of ivory and rose petals.</t39> <t40>A perfect Adonis, dear Basil, while you...</t40> <t41>Yes, you do have an intellectual expression, and so on, but beauty, true beauty, ends where the intellectual expression begins.</t41> <t42>The mind is a kind of exaggeration in itself, it disrupts the harmony of the face it enters.</t42> <t43>The moment a person starts to think, they become all nose, all forehead, or something equally dreadful.</t43> <t44>Just look at the men who have succeeded in intellectual professions.</t44> <t45>How utterly ugly they are from head to toe!</t45> <t46>Only the church is an exception to this, naturally.</t46> <t47>However, those who have made the church their profession do not think either.</t47> <t48>A bishop will say at eighty what he was taught to say at eighteen, and as a result, he always appears charming.</t48> <t49>As for that mysterious young friend of yours whose name you haven't mentioned but whose portrait truly fascinates me, I don't think he thinks at all.</t49> <t50>I can bet on it.</t50> <t51>A brainless, beautiful creature; he should be here in winter when we have no flowers to look at, and in summer when we need something to cool our minds.</t51> <t52>Don't flatter yourself, Basil, you don't resemble him in the slightest.</t52><t53>”</t53>
<t54>The painter replied, "You don't understand me, Harry."</t54> <t55>"Of course, I don't resemble him.</t55> <t56>I know this very well.</t56> <t57>In fact, I would be upset if I resembled him.</t57> <t58>Are you shrugging your shoulders?</t58> <t59>I'm telling you the truth.</t59> <t60>In my opinion, there is a curse in all physical and mental excellence, the kind of curse that has followed the faltering steps of kings throughout history.</t60> <t61>It is better not to be different from our other human brothers.</t61> <t62>I think the luckiest ones in this world are the ugly and the foolish.</t62> <t63>They can lie back and watch the game called life with their mouths open.</t63> <t64>Even if they don't know what victory is, at least they don't taste defeat either.</t64> <t65>They actually live as we all should live, carefree, indifferent, and without turmoil.</t65> <t66>They do not cause the ruin of others, nor do they become victims at the hands of others.</t66> <t67>Your title and wealth, Harry; my mind and intellect, however limited; if my art has any value; Dorian Gray's beauty...</t67> <t68>We will all suffer because of these gifts from God, and suffer greatly.</t68><t69>"</t69>
<t70>Lord Henry walked towards Basil Hallward, who was sitting on the other side of the studio, and said, "Dorian Gray?"</t70> <t71>"Is that his name?</t71><t72>"</t72>
<t73>“Yes, that's the name.</t73> <t74>I had no intention of telling you.</t74><t75>”</t75>
<t76>“Why, then?</t76><t77>”</t77>
<t78>“Oh, I can't explain it!</t78> <t79>If I like someone a lot, I never tell anyone their name.</t79> <t80>It feels like handing over a piece of their identity to someone else.</t80> <t81>Over time, I grew to love secrecy.</t81> <t82>It seems to me that this is the only thing that can make modern life appear mysterious and magical to us.</t82> <t83>If you keep it secret, even the most ordinary thing can become sweet and enjoyable.</t83> <t84>These days, when I leave the city, I don't tell anyone where I'm going.</t84> <t85>If I did, all the fun would be ruined.</t85> <t86>It might be a childish habit, but it seems to add a lot of romance to one's life.</t86> <t87>You probably think I'm really stupid because of this, don't you?</t87><t88>”</t88>
<t89>“Not at all,” replied Lord Henry.</t89> <t90>“Dear Basil, not at all.</t90> <t91>You forget that I am a married man; the only attractive aspect of marriage is that it makes a life of deception necessary for both parties.</t91> <t92>I have no idea where my wife is in the world, and my wife has no idea what I am up to.</t92> <t93>When we meet – which we occasionally do, when we are invited to a dinner together or go to see the duke – we tell each other the most ridiculous lies without batting an eye.</t93> <t94>My wife is very skilled at this, much more skilled than I am, to be honest.</t94> <t95>She never gets the dates wrong, whereas I always do.</t95> <t96>But when my wife catches my lie, she never makes a fuss.</t96> <t97>Sometimes I wish she would, but she just laughs it off.</t97><t98>”</t98>
<t99>Basil Hallward walked slowly towards the door leading to the garden and said, "I hate the way you talk about your marriage, Harry."</t99> <t100>"I believe that you are actually a very good husband, but you are ashamed of your own virtues.</t100> <t101>You are a unique fellow, indeed.</t101> <t102>You never say anything moral, nor do you ever do anything immoral.</t102> <t103>Your cynical attitude is nothing but an affectation.</t103><t104>"</t104>
<t105>Lord Henry laughed and said, "Being natural is just another pose, and the most irritating one at that, in my opinion."</t105>
<t106>Two young men went out to the garden together and sat on the long bamboo bench in the shade of a tall laurel tree.</t106> <t107>The sun was sliding down the polished leaves.</t107> <t108>The white daisies among the grass were trembling.</t108>
<t109>After a pause, Lord Henry took out his pocket watch.</t109>
<t110>“I'm sorry, but I have to go, Basil,” he murmured.</t110> <t111>“But before I go, I will insist that you answer one question.</t111> <t112>I asked you some time ago as well.</t112><t113>”</t113>
<t114>Without lifting his eyes from the ground, the painter asked, "What is it?"</t114>
<t115>“You know it very well.</t115><t116>”</t116>
<t117>“I don't know, Harry.</t117><t118>”</t118>
<t119>"Well then, let me tell you.</t119> <t120>I want you to explain why you don't want to exhibit Dorian Gray's portrait.</t120><t121>"</t121>
<t122>“I told you, the real reason.</t122><t123>”</t123>
<t124>“No, you didn't say that.</t124> <t125>You said, I put too much of myself into this painting.</t125> <t126>My friend, this is childhood.</t126><t127>”</t127>
<t128>Looking into his friend's eyes, Basil Hallward said, "Harry."</t128> <t129>"Every portrait that is painted with feeling is a portrait of the artist, not of the sitter.</t129> <t130>The sitter is merely the accident, the occasion.</t130> <t131>It is not he who is revealed by the painter; it is rather the painter who, on the colored canvas, reveals himself.</t131> <t132>That is the reason I will not exhibit this picture: I am afraid that I have shown in it the secret of my own soul."</t132><t133></t133>
<t134>Lord Henry laughed.</t134> <t135>“What could this mystery be?” he asked.</t135>
<t136>Hallward said, "I will tell you," but a look of astonishment had appeared on his face.</t136>
<t137>His friend glanced at him and said, "I'm waiting, Basil."</t137>
<t138>The painter replied, "There isn't much to tell."</t138> <t139>"I'm afraid you won't understand it properly.</t139> <t140>Maybe you won't want to believe it.</t140><t141>"</t141>
<t142>Lord Henry smiled and bent down, plucked a pink daisy from the grass, and began to examine it.</t142> <t143>Looking carefully at this small round flower with a golden eye in his hand, he said, "I have no doubt that I will understand."</t143> <t144>"As for believing; I can believe anything, as long as it is something utterly unbelievable.</t144><t145>"</t145>
<t146>The breeze scattered a few sprigs of flowers from the fruit trees; large lilacs, resembling clusters of stars, swayed in the languid air.</t146> <t147>A cricket began to chirp by the wall; a slender, long dragonfly, with its brown gauzy wings, flew past like a blue silk thread.</t147> <t148>Lord Henry felt as if he could hear his friend's heartbeat and wondered what he was going to say.</t148>
<t149>After a pause, the painter said, "It all comes down to this."</t149> <t150>"Two months ago, I attended one of Lady Brandon's gatherings.</t150> <t151>You know, we poor artists occasionally have to show up in high society, just to remind everyone that we are not complete savages.</t151> <t152>Once, you told me; as long as one wears an evening coat and a white tie, anyone, even a stockbroker, can gain a reputation as a civilized person.</t152> <t153>Anyway, I had been in the room for about ten minutes.</t153> <t154>I was chatting with those large, overly adorned, elderly noblewomen and those tedious academy members when suddenly, I felt someone was looking at me.</t154> <t155>When I turned around, I saw Dorian Gray.</t155> <t156>I didn't know him at all.</t156> <t157>When our eyes met, I felt my face turn pale.</t157> <t158>A strange unease enveloped me.</t158> <t159>The mere presence of this person I was facing was so captivating that, if I allowed it, he could take over my entire being, my soul, even my art.</t159> <t160>I realized this immediately.</t160> <t161>Yet, I did not want any external interference in my life.</t161> <t162>You know how independent I am by nature, Harry.</t162> <t163>I have always been my own master.</t163> <t164>That is, until I met Dorian Gray.</t164> <t165>At that moment...</t165> <t166>But I don't know how to explain it to you.</t166> <t167>It was as if a voice was telling me that I was on the brink of a terrible crisis.</t167> <t168>I had a premonition that fate had prepared unparalleled joys and unparalleled sorrows for me.</t168>
<t169>I got scared, turned around, and walked to the door with the intention of going outside. It wasn't the voice of my conscience that made me do this, it was a kind of cowardice.</t169> <t170>I'm not giving myself any credit for trying to escape.</t170><t171>”</t171>
<t172>“Conscience and cowardice are really the same thing, Basil.</t172> <t173>Conscience is just the trade name of the firm, that's all.</t173><t174>”</t174>
<t175>“I don't believe this, Harry; I don't believe you think this way either.</t175> <t176>Anyway, whatever it was that made me act this way...</t176> <t177>maybe it was pride, I used to be very proud once...</t177> <t178>The truth is, I made my way through the crowd towards the door.</t178> <t179>But there, I naturally collided with Lady Brandon.</t179> <t180>Lady Brandon exclaimed, ‘Mr. Hallward, are you running away so early?’</t180> <t181>You know how oddly high-pitched her voice is!</t181><t182>”</t182>
<t183>Lord Henry, plucking the daisy with his long, sensitive fingers, said, "Yes, he is a peacock in everything but beauty."</t183>
<t184>“I couldn't escape from her.</t184> <t185>She introduced me to people of royal lineage, to those with titles and decorations, to hook-nosed individuals with enormous crowns, and to elderly ladies.</t185> <t186>She would refer to me as her very dear friend.</t186> <t187>Although we had only met once before, she was determined to take me under her wing.</t187> <t188>I suppose one of my paintings had gained some fame at that time, or rather, it had been written about in the colorful boulevard newspapers, which is the measure of immortality in the nineteenth century.</t188> <t189>I came face to face with the young man who had stirred such strange emotions within me.</t189> <t190>Our eyes met.</t190> <t191>It was a reckless move, I know, but I asked Lady Brandon to introduce us.</t191> <t192>Perhaps it wasn't recklessness, who knows.</t192> <t193>Maybe it was inevitable.</t193> <t194>Even if we hadn't been introduced, we would have spoken to each other.</t194> <t195>I'm sure of it.</t195> <t196>Later, Dorian said the same thing.</t196> <t197>He also felt that it was written in our fate to know each other.</t197><t198>”</t198>
<t199>Lord Henry asked, "Well, how did Lady Brandon describe this wonderful young man?"</t199> <t200>"I know she is excellent at giving quick summaries of all her guests.</t200> <t201>I remember once she took me to a red-faced, grumpy old man covered in medals and ribbons; then she leaned over and began to whisper tragic details into my ear, which I'm sure everyone there could clearly hear.</t201> <t202>Lady Brandon treats her guests the way auctioneers view the items they sell.</t202> <t203>She either dismisses them with a single remark or reveals everything about them, except what one actually wants to know.</t203><t204>"</t204>
<t205>The painter spoke with a bit of boredom, "Poor Lady Brandon! You are not showing her any mercy, Harry!"</t205>
<t206>“My dear friend, this woman attempted to establish a salon but only managed to set up a restaurant.</t206> <t207>How can I admire her?</t207> <t208>But you tell me, what did she say about Mr. Dorian Gray?</t208><t209>”</t209>
<t210>“He mumbled something like: a lovely child...</t210> <t211>We were inseparable with his poor mother.</t211> <t212>I can't remember what Dorian was doing...</t212> <t213>I'm afraid he's doing nothing...</t213> <t214>Ah, yes, he plays the piano, doesn't he...</t214> <t215>Or was it the violin, dear Mr. Gray?..</t215> <t216>We both couldn't help but laugh, and we immediately hit it off.</t216><t217>”</t217>
<t218>The Young Lord plucked another daisy and said, "Laughter is not a bad start for friendship; it is the best way to end it."</t218>
<t219>Hallward shook his head, "Harry, you don't understand what friendship means," he said.</t219> <t220>"Nor do you know the meaning of enmity.</t220> <t221>You love everyone, which means you are indifferent to everyone.</t221><t222>"</t222>
<t223>Lord Henry, "What a terrible injustice you do to me!" he said, pushing his hat back; he looked at the clouds flying like unraveled bright silk skeins over the turquoise dome of the summer sky.</t223> <t224>"Yes, you do me a terrible injustice.</t224> <t225>In fact, I do distinguish between people.</t225> <t226>I choose my friends for their beauty, my acquaintances for the solidity of their character, and my enemies for their sharp intellect.</t226> <t227>One cannot be too careful in choosing one's enemies.</t227> <t228>I do not have a single foolish enemy.</t228> <t229>All my enemies have strong mental faculties, and therefore they appreciate my worth.</t229> <t230>Do you find me very arrogant?</t230> <t231>Yes, I am quite arrogant.</t231><t232>"</t232>
<t233>“I think so too, Harry.</t233> <t234>But according to your classification, I am merely an acquaintance in your eyes.</t234><t235>”</t235>
<t236>“My very dear friend Basil, you are much more than an acquaintance.</t236><t237>”</t237>
<t238>“But far from being just a friend.</t238> <t239>Am I a kind of sibling, I wonder?</t239><t240>”</t240>
<t241>“Oh, don't call me brother!</t241> <t242>I don't like that word at all.</t242> <t243>My older brother doesn't seem to know how to die; and my younger siblings don't seem to know anything but dying.</t243><t244>”</t244>
<t245>Basil Hallward frowned and said, "Harry!"</t245>
<t246>“My friend, I'm joking.</t246> <t247>Still, I can't help but hate my relatives.</t247> <t248>It's probably because none of us can stand seeing our own flaws in others.</t248> <t249>I understand very well the British democracy's grudge against what they call the bad traits of the upper classes.</t249> <t250>The masses want drunkenness, stupidity, and immorality to be exclusive to them!</t250> <t251>When one of us disgraces themselves, it's as if their private property has been violated: The public's outrage was truly remarkable when poor little Southwark found himself in the divorce court.</t251> <t252>Yet, I don't think even one-tenth of the proletariat lives a flawless life.</t252><t253>”</t253>
<t254>“Harry, I don't believe a single word you're saying.</t254> <t255>Moreover, I'm sure you don't believe it either.</t255><t256>”</t256>
<t257>Lord Henry was stroking his sharp, chestnut-colored beard and kept tapping his patent leather shoes with his tasseled ebony cane.</t257>
<t258>“How typically English you are, Basil! You are stating the same opinion for the second time.</t258> <t259>When a person presents an idea to a true Englishman...</t259> <t260>Which is already a dangerous task...</t260> <t261>He never even considers whether the idea is right or wrong.</t261> <t262>The only thing he deems important is whether he himself believes in it.</t262> <t263>Now, there is no connection between the value of an idea and the sincerity of the person presenting it.</t263> <t264>To be honest, most likely, the less sincere a person is, the purer the intellectual value of the idea will be, because it will not be tainted by that person's needs, desires, and prejudices.</t264> <t265>However, I have no intention of discussing politics, sociology, or metaphysics with you.</t265> <t266>I love people more than principles.</t266> <t267>Tell me a bit more about this Mr. Dorian Gray.</t267> <t268>Do you see him often?</t268><t269>”</t269>
<t270>“Every day.</t270> <t271>If I don't see him every day, I can't be happy!</t271> <t272>I can't do without him anymore.</t272><t273>”</t273>
<t274>“An unusual thing, indeed!</t274> <t275>I thought you didn't care about anything other than art.</t275><t276>”</t276>
<t277>The painter replied seriously, "For me, my art means everything now."</t277> <t278>"Harry, sometimes I think there are only two periods in world history that can be considered important, in my opinion.</t278> <t279>The first is the emergence of a new art environment, and the second is the appearance of a new personality for art.</t279> <t280>The invention of oil painting was to the Venetian painters what the face of Antinous was to the late Greek sculptors, and one day, Dorian Gray's face will hold that importance for me.</t280> <t281>I'm not just drawing and painting because of him.</t281> <t282>Of course, I am doing those things.</t282> <t283>Yet, in my eyes, he means much more than just a model.</t283> <t284>I'm not going to tell you that I'm not satisfied with the paintings I made inspired by him, or that art cannot capture his beauty.</t284> <t285>There is nothing that art cannot express; I know that the works I've done since I met Dorian Gray are good products, the best I've ever produced.</t285> <t286>Still, how strange...</t286> <t287>I wonder if you'll understand me?..</t287> <t288>Thanks to his personality, I feel as if there's a completely new style in art.</t288> <t289>A completely new style.</t289> <t290>I see everything differently, I perceive everything differently now.</t290> <t291>Nowadays, I can create life in a completely new form that I didn't know or see before.</t291> <t292>'A form dreamt of in the days of thought.'</t292> <t293>Who said that?</t293> <t294>I can't remember, but that's what Dorian Gray is to me.</t294> <t295>The mere visible presence of this boy...</t295> <t296>Actually, he's over twenty, but I always see him as a child...</t296> <t297>Yes, even his mere visible presence...</t297> <t298>Oh, I don't know if you can grasp the full meaning of all this?</t298> <t299>Without even knowing it, this boy seems to define the lines of a completely new art school: a school that will combine all the fire of the romantic school with all the perfection of the ancient Greek school.</t299> <t300>The harmony of body and soul...</t300> <t301>How important this is!</t301> <t302>We, in our stupidity, have separated them, invented a crude realism, an empty idealism.</t302> <t303>Oh, Harry, if only you could understand what Dorian Gray symbolizes to me!</t303> <t304>You know that landscape of mine, Agnew offered a fortune for it, but I didn't let it go.</t304> <t305>It's one of my best works.</t305> <t306>And why, do you think?</t306> <t307>Because while I was painting it, Dorian Gray was sitting beside me.</t307> <t308>There must have been an intangible influence from him to me, because for the first time in my life, I found the magic I had always sought but never caught in the ordinary forest in front of me.</t308><t309>"</t309>
<t310>"Basil, this is unheard of!</t310> <t311>I absolutely must see Dorian Gray.</t311><t312>"</t312>
<t313>Hallward stood up and began pacing back and forth in the garden.</t313> <t314>A little later, he came back again.</t314> <t315>“Harry,” he said.</t315> <t316>“Dorian Gray is merely a motive that drives me to creativity.</t316> <t317>I find everything in him.</t317> <t318>You may find nothing.</t318> <t319>The times when he is most present in my works are also the times when his image is not seen.</t319> <t320>As I said, he is the intuition of a completely new style.</t320> <t321>I find him in the curve of some lines, in the beauty of certain colors, in their indescribable delicacy.</t321> <t322>That's all.</t322><t323>”</t323>
<t324>Lord Henry asked, "Then why don't you exhibit his portrait?"</t324>
<t325>“It is because I have unconsciously reflected in that portrait the strange artistic idolatry I have heard of.</t325> <t326>I never spoke to him about these feelings, naturally.</t326> <t327>Dorian Gray has no idea about it.</t327> <t328>Nor will he ever.</t328> <t329>However, others might sense it; and I have no intention of exposing my soul to their shallow, curious eyes.</t329> <t330>I will never lay my heart under their microscopes.</t330> <t331>There is too much of myself in the portrait, Harry, far too much!</t331><t332>”</t332>
<t333>“Poets do not split hairs as much as you do.</t333> <t334>They know that passion is something useful to publish.</t334> <t335>Nowadays, a burning heart can make countless impressions.</t335><t336>”</t336>
<t337>The painter shouted, "I despise them for this very reason!"</t337> <t338>"An artist should create beautiful things, but should not put anything of his own life into them.</t338> <t339>We live in an age where everyone looks at art as a kind of autobiography.</t339> <t340>We have lost the sense of abstract beauty.</t340> <t341>One day I will show the world what it is; that is why the world will never see my portrait of Dorian Gray.</t341><t342>"</t342>

<t1044>Third chapter</t1044>
<t1045>The next day at half-past twelve, Lord Henry Wotton left Curzon Street and headed towards the Albany Hotel to visit his uncle, Lord Fermor.</t1045> <t1046>The world, which could not derive any benefit from Lord Fermor, who was a cheerful, talkative, and friendly old man despite some of his blunt behaviors, accused him of selfishness.</t1046> <t1047>However, society considered him generous because Lord Fermor would feed those who entertained him.</t1047> <t1048>His father had been the Ambassador to Madrid during Isabella's youth, long before Prim was even thought of, but he had quit the diplomatic profession in a fit of anger when he was not made Ambassador to Paris.</t1048> <t1049>Lord Fermor believed he fully deserved this position due to his lineage, mischief, the reports he prepared, his eloquence, and his excessive love of pleasure and luxury.</t1049>
<t1050>His son, who was acting as his secretary, had also submitted his resignation along with him.</t1050> <t1051>It was thought that he had been a bit foolish at the time.</t1051> <t1052>A few months later, the new Lord Fermor, who inherited his father's title, dedicated himself to seriously studying that magnificent art of aristocracy, namely the art of doing absolutely nothing.</t1052> <t1053>He had two huge houses in the city, but preferred to stay in a hotel suite because it was less troublesome, and usually had his meals there as well.</t1053> <t1054>He took a slight interest in managing the coal mines he owned around the Midlands, and to excuse the stain on his honor for making this effort, he would say, "There is only one benefit of coal: it provides the upper class with the privilege of burning wood in their own fireplaces!"</t1054>
<t1055>Politically, he was a Tory, except when the Tories were in power.</t1055> <t1056>During such times, Lord Fermor would denounce them as a "pack of Radicals" and condemn them vehemently.</t1056> <t1057>In the eyes of his personal valet, who managed him with an overbearing authority, Lord Fermor was a hero, while to his relatives, whom he managed with the same overbearing authority, he was a tyrant.</t1057> <t1058>Only England could have produced him, and he was always saying that the country was going to the dogs.</t1058> <t1059>His principles might have been somewhat outdated, but there was no denying his prejudices.</t1059>
<t1060>When Lord Henry entered, he found his uncle grumbling while reading the Times newspaper, puffing on a leaf cigar, and wearing a sports jacket.</t1060>
<t1061>"Well, Harry," said the old man.</t1061> <t1062>"What are you doing here so early?</t1062> <t1063>I thought you high-society fops wouldn't get out of bed before two and wouldn't show your faces before five.</t1063><t1064>"</t1064>
<t1065>“Believe me, Uncle George, it is purely familial love that brought me here.</t1065> <t1066>I want to get something from you!</t1066><t1067>”</t1067>
<t1068>Lord Fermor grimaced, "It must be about money," he said.</t1068> <t1069>"Anyway, sit down and tell me about it.</t1069> <t1070>Young people these days think that money is everything in life.</t1070><t1071>"</t1071>
<t1072>Lord Henry murmured, "Yes."</t1072> <t1073>"They know this to be true even when they grow old.</t1073> <t1074>However, what I want is not money.</t1074> <t1075>Only those who pay their debts want money, dear Uncle George, whereas I never pay my debts.</t1075> <t1076>The capital of the younger sons of nobles is credit, and one can live quite pleasantly with this credit.</t1076> <t1077>Besides, I always do business with the Dartmoor tradesmen, so they never bother me.</t1077> <t1078>No, what I want is knowledge.</t1078> <t1079>Not useful knowledge, of course, but useless knowledge.</t1079><t1080>"</t1080>
<t1081>"Well, Harry, I can relay to you any information you want from the Blue Book of England.</t1081> <t1082>Although, nowadays, the guys who write it have gone crazy.</t1082> <t1083>When I was in the diplomatic service, things were more orderly.</t1083> <t1084>But now, from what I hear, they even hire people there through exams.</t1084> <t1085>That thing called an exam, my friend, is nonsense from start to finish.</t1085> <t1086>If a person is a gentleman from the polite society, what he knows is more than enough for him.</t1086> <t1087>If he's not a gentleman, everything he knows is harmful to him.</t1087><t1088>"</t1088>
<t1089>Lord Henry said in his lazy voice, "Mr. Dorian Gray has no place in the Blue Book, Uncle George."</t1089>

<t1509>Fourth Chapter</t1509>
<t1510>One afternoon, a month later, Dorian Gray was sitting, sunk into an extremely comfortable chair, in the small library of Lord Henry's house in Mayfair.</t1510> <t1511>This room, paneled with oak painted in olive color, with cream-colored cornices and ceiling decorations, and scattered with long-tasseled silk Persian prayer rugs on a brick-red carpet that covered the floor wall-to-wall, had a unique charm of its own.</t1511> <t1512>On a tiny table made of yellow Indian wood stood a small statue by Clodion, and next to it, a copy of Les Cent Nouvelles (The One Hundred New Stories).</t1512> <t1513>This book had been bound by Clovis Eve for Margaret of Valois, with gilded daisies chosen by the Queen herself scattered over it.</t1513> <t1514>On the mantelpiece, large vases of blue porcelain held tulips of various colors; the apricot-colored light of a summer day in London filtered through the small leaded panes of the window.</t1514>
<t1515>Lord Henry had not arrived yet.</t1515> <t1516>He was deliberately late everywhere, as it was his principle to consider punctuality a theft of time.</t1516> <t1517>Therefore, Dorian Gray was sulking quite a bit as he reluctantly flipped through a very intricately illustrated copy of Manon Lescaut he found on one of the shelves.</t1517> <t1518>The monotonous ticking of the Louis Quatorze clock was also getting on his nerves.</t1518> <t1519>A couple of times, he even considered getting up and leaving.</t1519>
<t1520>Finally, he heard footsteps outside; the door opened.</t1520>
<t1521>Dorian complained, "But you're late, Harry!"</t1521>

<t2005>When Dorian went out, Lord Henry closed his heavy eyelids and began to ponder.</t2005> <t2006>The fact was that there were very few people who interested him as much as Dorian Gray did.</t2006> <t2007>Yet, the young lord felt not the slightest jealousy or annoyance that the boy was madly infatuated with someone else.</t2007> <t2008>On the contrary, he found it pleasing.</t2008> <t2009>This passion made Dorian Gray more interesting, more worthy of study.</t2009> <t2010>Lord Henry had always harbored a deep curiosity about the natural sciences, though he found their ordinary subjects trivial and insignificant.</t2010> <t2011>Thus, he began by examining himself under the microscope, eventually turning to dissect and study others.</t2011> <t2012>Human life: this was the only thing worth examining in Lord Henry's eyes.</t2012> <t2013>Nothing could be as valuable.</t2013> <t2014>Indeed, while the observer watched life in the test tube filled with a mixture of pains and joys, he could not wear a glass mask, nor could he prevent the poisonous vapors from affecting his brain, causing his imagination to throb with strange fantasies and shapeless dreams.</t2014> <t2015>There were such insidious poisons that one had to be poisoned by them to discern their qualities.</t2015> <t2016>Yet, in the end, how magnificent a reward this examination gave to a person!</t2016> <t2017>How marvelous the world became in one's eyes!</t2017> <t2018>To notice the strange, rigid logic of passion and the experiences of the mind tinged with sentimentality, to observe where these two converged, where they diverged, where they reconciled, and where they clashed...</t2018> <t2019>What a great delight was hidden in this!</t2019> <t2020>Who cared about the cost?</t2020> <t2021>No excitement's price was too high.</t2021>

<t2047>Lord Henry had begun to wonder: Was it within our power to turn psychology into an absolutely precise and definitive science, thereby ensuring that even the smallest springs of life were laid bare?</t2047> <t2048>In that case, we were always misunderstanding ourselves and rarely understanding others.</t2048> <t2049>What was called experience had no ethical value.</t2049> <t2050>It was merely a name people gave to their mistakes.</t2050> <t2051>Moralists generally considered it a kind of warning, claimed it had an ethical benefit in the development of personality, and boasted that it was a sign of what to follow and what to avoid.</t2051> <t2052>However, there was no driving force in experience.</t2052> <t2053>The active role it played was as insignificant as the role of conscience.</t2053> <t2054>The only truth it revealed was that our future would be equal to our past; we would commit the sin we once committed with disgust many times in the future, and this time with pleasure.</t2054>

<t4481>The coppery green sky, adorned with a single star, and the shimmer of the cloudless sky shone through the window.</t4481> <t4482>Dorian continued to read in this pale light until his eyes could no longer see.</t4482> <t4483>Victor warned his master about the time on numerous occasions.</t4483> <t4484>Finally, Dorian stood up, went to the next room, placed the book on the small Florentine table beside his bed, and began to change his clothes to go out for dinner.</t4484>
<t4485>It was almost nine o'clock when he reached the lodge.</t4485> <t4486>He found Lord Henry alone in the morning room.</t4486> <t4487>It was clear that he was very bored.</t4487>

<t4497>Lord Henry murmured, "So, you have learned this?"</t4497>

<t4499>Chapter Eleven</t4499>
<t4500>Dorian Gray could not free himself from the influence of this book for years.</t4500> <t4501>Or perhaps it would be more accurate to say that he never even thought of freeing himself from this influence.</t4501> <t4502>He bought exactly nine large copies of the book from Paris.</t4502> <t4503>He had them bound in different colors to match the changes in his mood and the whims of his often erratic nature.</t4503> <t4504>The hero of the novel, this extraordinary young Parisian man who combined emotional and scientific tendencies within himself, had become a symbol for Dorian, as if he had been designed before Dorian was born.</t4504> <t4505>In any case, the entire book seemed like a story of his life, written before it was lived.</t4505>
<t4506>At one point, he was luckier than the incredible hero of the novel himself.</t4506> <t4507>Upon the sudden ruin of her beauty, which was evidently very striking at first, the Parisian had developed a rather ridiculous fear of mirrors, polished metals, and still waters at a very young age.</t4507> <t4508>However, Dorian had never known this fear, and there was no reason for him to know it.</t4508> <t4509>He was reading the final chapters, which told the somewhat exaggerated but truly heartbreaking story of a man who had lost what he valued most in the world and in people, with an almost cruel delight.</t4509> <t4510>Perhaps in every delight, just as in every pleasure, there is a share of cruelty.</t4510>
<t4511>Dorian Gray's extraordinary beauty, which fascinated Basil Hallward and many others like him, seemed never to diminish.</t4511> <t4512>Even those who had heard the ugliest things about him – for from time to time, incredible rumors about his lifestyle would circulate around London and become the talk of the clubs – did not believe he could be capable of baseness when they saw him.</t4512> <t4513>Dorian always had an appearance as if he had kept himself clean and untouched by the world's filth.</t4513> <t4514>Those who spoke with foul mouths would fall silent when he entered.</t4514> <t4515>There was something in the pure serenity of Dorian Gray's face that seemed to reproach them.</t4515> <t4516>His mere presence was enough to evoke in such people the memory of their own tarnished innocence.</t4516> <t4517>They wondered how someone so charming, beautiful, and refined could remain untouched by the grime of this age, which was both opportunistic and lustful.</t4517>
<t4518>After his long, mysterious absences that led to strange rumors among his friends—or those who thought they were his friends—Dorian Gray would quietly go up to the room upstairs when he returned home, unlock the door with the key he now always kept with him, take a mirror in hand, and stand before the portrait painted by Basil Hallward.</t4518> <t4519>He would look at the aged, evil-filled face on the canvas, and then at his own beautiful, fresh face smiling back at him from the mirrored glass.</t4519> <t4520>The sharp contrast between the two would stir a thrill of pleasure within him.</t4520> <t4521>He was falling more and more in love with his own beauty, and becoming more and more interested in the decay of his soul.</t4521> <t4522>He would examine with infinite attention and a dreadful, inhuman delight the lines that played around the corners of that full, sensual mouth and the furrows that marred the wrinkled forehead.</t4522> <t4523>Often, he did not know which was more terrifying: the marks of sin or the marks of aging?</t4523> <t4524>He would smile as he held his own white hand next to the portrait's wrinkled, swollen hands, mocking that misshapen body and those emaciated legs.</t4524>
<t4525>On some nights, lying sleepless in his own fragrant room or in the small, filthy room of the notorious inn at the dock where he came and went under an assumed name, he would think with a pity –sharpened even more because it was entirely selfish– about how he was deliberately driving his soul to ruin.</t4525> <t4526>However, such moments were very rare.</t4526> <t4527>The curiosity about life that Lord Henry had first awakened while they were sitting in Basil’s garden seemed to flare up as it was satisfied.</t4527> <t4528>The more Dorian knew, the more he wanted to learn.</t4528> <t4529>He had a ravenous appetite that grew more voracious as it was sated.</t4529>
<t4530>However, it could not be said that he behaved excessively recklessly, at least in his relations with society.</t4530> <t4531>In winter, once or twice a month, and every Wednesday evening during the social season, he would open his beautiful house to those around him and invite the most famous musicians of the day to enchant his guests with their artistic wonders.</t4531> <t4532>Those small dinner parties, in the organization of which Lord Henry always helped him, were famous not only for the meticulousness shown in the selection and placement of the guests but also for the refined taste displayed in the decoration of the table.</t4532> <t4533>The decoration of these tables with antique gold and silver sets, embroidered cloths, and flowers from distant climates resembled a symphony.</t4533> <t4534>Among the guests, especially those who were still in their youth, many saw or imagined Dorian Gray as the ideal type they had dreamed of during their school days at Eton and Oxford: a type that combined the true culture of a book-loving scholar with all the agility, elegance, and impeccable manners of a "man of life."</t4534> <t4535>To such people, Dorian appeared as one of those whom Dante described as "those who seek perfection by worshipping beauty."</t4535> <t4536>Like Gautier, he was also one of those who believed in the existence of the visible world.</t4536>
<t4537>Indeed, for him, the foremost and most sublime of all arts was Life itself; all other arts were nothing but a preparation for Life.</t4537> <t4538>In fashion, which for a moment made the unreal universal, and in dandyism, which was his own emphasis on the absolute contemporaneity of beauty, Dorian did not fail to find something appealing.</t4538> <t4539>His style of dress and the fashions he occasionally adopted left a noticeable impact on the young men at the Mayfair balls and the Pall Mall Club.</t4539> <t4540>These young men imitated his every move, trying to capture the charm and attractiveness that were the by-products of his elegant affectations, which he did not actually take very seriously.</t4540>
<t4541>Although Dorian eagerly settled into the position that opened up before him as soon as he reached adolescence.</t4541> <t4542>In fact, he did not lack a secret pleasure at the thought that he could play the role in London that the author of “Satyricon” once played in Nero's Rome.</t4542> <t4543>Yet, deep in his heart, there was a desire to be something more than just an arbiter elegantiarum – an arbiter of elegance to be consulted on matters such as wearing jewelry, tying a cravat, or holding a cane.</t4543> <t4544>He was trying to develop a brand-new life scheme that found the highest realization of oneself in the spiritualization of the senses, with a rational philosophy and regular principles.</t4544>
<t4545>The sanctification of the senses has often, and very rightly, been condemned and criticized.</t4545> <t4546>Mankind has always harbored a natural dread of passions and senses that appear stronger than oneself (and are known to be shared with lower forms of life).</t4546> <t4547>However, it seemed to Dorian Gray that the true nature of the senses had never been understood.</t4547> <t4548>The senses have been forced to remain wild and animalistic because the world has aimed to crush them by starving or to kill them through suffering.</t4548> <t4549>Yet, they should have become elements of a new spirituality, whose dominant characteristic is a refined intuition of beauty.</t4549>
<t4550>Dorian, as he looked back and examined the progress of humanity throughout history, felt a sense of loss.</t4550> <t4551>What had mankind given up!</t4551> <t4552>And all for nothing!</t4552> <t4553>Mad, stubborn denials rooted in fear, turning away, monstrous self-inflicted tortures, deprivations.</t4553> <t4554>Mankind had done these things out of ignorance, supposedly to avoid shame and degradation; yet the result of their choice had been a degradation far worse than what they had tried to escape.</t4554> <t4555>With its marvelous irony, nature had forced those who had withdrawn from the world to live alongside wild desert animals, giving monks the animals of the fields as companions.</t4555>
<t4556>Yes: Just as Lord Henry predicted, a new hedonism would emerge and recreate life anew; it is very strange that it would save life from the harsh, unattractive conservatism that has resurfaced today.</t4556> <t4557>During this new hedonism, it would naturally make use of thought.</t4557> <t4558>However, no theory or system that required the abandonment of passions and excitements would be adopted.</t4558> <t4559>Rather, its purpose would be to directly experience such sensations, not the bittersweet fruits of these life experiences.</t4559> <t4560>This movement would be unaware of both the aesthetism that freezes the senses and the vulgar debauchery that dulls them.</t4560> <t4561>It would teach people to focus on the moments of a life that consists of a single moment.</t4561>
<t4562>Among us, there are very few who do not wake up suddenly at dawn after those dreamless sleeps that almost push us to embrace death, or after a night filled with terrors and monstrous joys.</t4562> <t4563>In such nights, ghosts seep into the compartments of the mind that are even more frightening than realities, filled with the life force hidden in all monsters.</t4563> <t4564>It is this life force in the monsters that gives Gothic art its unending vitality.</t4564> <t4565>Isn't Gothic art actually considered a branch of art unique to those whose minds are afflicted with dream fever?</t4565>
<t4566>Yes, at such moments, white fingers gently slip inside through the curtains.</t4566> <t4567>These fingers seem to be trembling.</t4567> <t4568>And they seem to make the curtains ripple.</t4568> <t4569>Silent, dark shadows, cloaked in surreal forms, crawl and settle into the corners of the room.</t4569> <t4570>Outside, there is the rustling of birds among the leaves, and the sounds of people heading to work can be heard.</t4570> <t4571>The wind descends from the hills, and although it hesitates to wake those sleeping in the silent house, it roams around as if compelled to draw sleep out of its purple cave, sighing and sobbing.</t4571> <t4572>Dark, thin veils peel away one after another, opening step by step, objects regain their usual colors and shapes, and we watch as dawn recreates the world according to that ancient pattern.</t4572> <t4573>Pale reflections return to the mirrors.</t4573> <t4574>The flameless candelabras remain where we left them, and beside them, the half-opened book we read at night, the flower with a wired stem we pinned to our lapel at the ball, or the letter we are afraid to read—perhaps one we've read a hundred times.</t4574> <t4575>Nothing appears to have changed to our eyes.</t4575> <t4576>From the surreal shadows of the night, the real life we know emerges.</t4576> <t4577>We must pick it up from where we left off.</t4577>
<t4578>Then, the necessity to continue expending energy to turn the tiresome wheel of the same entrenched habits descends upon us like a cloud.</t4578> <t4579>We might perhaps be seized by a mad desire to open our eyes to a world reshaped solely for our pleasure in the darkness of night: a world where everything carries brand new colors and shapes, has changed, and contains other mysteries; a world that almost leaves no place for the past, where the conscious responsibility and regret of the past leave no residue, where joyful memories do not bring disappointment, and sweet memories do not bring bitterness.</t4579>
<t4580>It seemed to Dorian Gray that the true purpose of living –or one of the true purposes– was to be able to create worlds like these.</t4580> <t4581>Chasing after thrills that were both new and pleasurable, and possessed the "extraordinary" qualities he considered indispensable elements of romantic adventures, Dorian was actually adopting certain modes of thought that he knew were alien to his nature, allowing himself to be influenced by them indirectly.</t4581> <t4582>Then, so to speak, after absorbing their color and satisfying his intellectual curiosity, he would abandon them with a strange indifference.</t4582> <t4583>Such indifference is not at all unusual in a passionate character; in fact, according to some contemporary psychologists, such a disposition is considered a prerequisite for such a temperament.</t4583>
<t4584>There were rumors circulating that Dorian Gray was about to convert to the Catholic Church at one point.</t4584> <t4585>The fact is that the methods of this sect had always seemed very attractive to him.</t4585> <t4586>The Roman Catholic Church, with its daily sacrifices that it found more magnificent than all the sacrifices of the ancient world, its outright denial of the senses, as well as the simplicity of its elements and the unchanging inner anguish of the Human Tragedy it sought to symbolize, stirred Dorian's emotions.</t4586> <t4587>He enjoyed watching the priest in his tall, embroidered robe, kneeling on the cold marble floor: the priest opening the temple curtain with his white hands, cutting the sacred bread into the chalice in the guise of the Mass of the Passion of Man, and striking his chest for his sins, lifting the jewel-encrusted, lantern-shaped sacred bread container with the pale slice of bread inside it into the air.</t4587> <t4588>Sometimes Dorian was inclined to believe that this was indeed panis caelestis, the bread of angels.</t4588> <t4589>The incense burners that the children in lace and red garments, with serious expressions, tossed into the air like large, gilded flowers, subtly enchanted him.</t4589> <t4590>As he left the church, he would look curiously at those dark confession booths, and he would want to sit in the dim shadows of one of them, listening to the men and women whispering the true stories of their lives from behind the leaf screens.</t4590>
<t4591>However, he never made the mistake of officially adopting any religion or system that would restrict his mental development.</t4591> <t4592>He never fell into the error of mistaking a shelter for one night, or even an inn to take refuge in for a few hours of a night when the stars did not shine and the moon did not rise, for a house to live in.</t4592> <t4593>Mysticism, which has a wonderful effect of making ordinary things appear extraordinary to our eyes, and the mysterious antinomianism6 movement that often follows it for some reason, occupied him for a season.</t4593> <t4594>In another season, Dorian inclined towards the materialistic teachings of the Darwinismus7 movement in Germany.</t4594> <t4595>He took a strange pleasure in tracing the thoughts and feelings of a person to a pearly cell in the brain or a white nerve in the body.</t4595> <t4596>The idea that the soul was entirely dependent on certain bodily conditions—whether diseased or healthy, normal or abnormal—gave him satisfaction.</t4596>
<t4597>The thing is, as we mentioned earlier, no theory about life seemed as important to him as life itself.</t4597> <t4598>He was well aware of how barren intellectual theories could be when they were disconnected from action and experience.</t4598> <t4599>He knew that the senses also had spiritual mysteries to reveal, which were no less significant than those of the soul.</t4599>
<t4600>Thus, you see him studying perfumes, the secrets of perfume making, distilling fragrant oils, and burning aromatic resins from the Far East.</t4600><t4601>He observed that every change in the mind had a counterpart in sensory life and strove to uncover the true connection between them: What was it in the daily scent that made one mystical?</t4601><t4602>What was it in amber that inflamed emotions, in the scent of violets that revived memories of lost loves, in musk that disturbed the mind, in champaca that stimulated the imagination?</t4602><t4603>Dorian was developing a solid philosophy about perfumes and attempting to measure their various effects, studying fragrant roots, flowers laden with aromatic pollen, and melissas; fragrant, dense trees: the sickening hyacinths, the delirium-inducing hovenias, the aloe tree said to banish melancholy from the soul...</t4603>
<t4604>At one point, he devoted himself entirely to music.</t4604> <t4605>He organized very peculiar concerts in a long room with latticed windows, a ceiling painted red and gilded, and walls lacquered in olive green.</t4605> <t4606>In these recitals, wild Gypsies would extract wandering melodies from their small, multi-stringed instruments, dignified Tunisians wrapped in yellow shawls would pluck the taut strings of their enormous ouds, grinning black men would pound their copper drums with monotonous beats, and thin Indians with turbans on their heads, squatting on red prayer rugs, would enchant or pretend to enchant terrifying, long, poisonous, horned snakes by playing their long flutes made of reed or brass.</t4606> <t4607>When he couldn't be moved by Schubert's delicate harmony, Chopin's exquisite melancholy, or even Beethoven's magnificent melodies, the harsh intervals and shrill dissonances of barbaric music excited Dorian.</t4607> <t4608>He had collected the strangest instruments he could find from all corners of the world: from the tombs of vanished nations, from places where a handful of wild tribes had managed to survive after coming into contact with Western civilization.</t4608> <t4609>He loved to caress and try them out.</t4609> <t4610>He had acquired those mysterious Juruparis of the Rio Negro Indians, which women were not allowed to look at and young men could only see after undergoing fasting and flogging trials.</t4610> <t4611>He had the earthenware flutes of the Peruvians that chirped with bird-like high pitches, the flutes made of human bone that Alfonso de Ovalle heard in Chile, and the resonant jade stones found around Cuzco that produced indescribably beautiful sounds...</t4611> <t4612>He had painted gourds filled with pebbles that rattled when shaken; the long clarinets of the Mexicans that were played by sucking rather than blowing; the harsh-sounding turé of the Amazon natives, said to be heard from three leagues away, played by the sentinels who kept watch in the high trees all day; the teponaztli, carved from wood with two vibrating tongues, played with sticks coated in a gum made from plant latex; the yotl rattles of the Aztecs, clustered like grape bunches; and that huge drum seen by Bernal Diaz in the Mexican temple he entered with Cortez, which he vividly described for its mournful sound.</t4612> <t4613>Gray, captivated by the variety and striking nature of these instruments, took a peculiar pleasure in thinking that, like nature, art also created monsters, animal-shaped, and ugly-sounding things.</t4613>
<t4614>However, after a while, he grew tired of these as well, and found himself either alone or with Lord Henry in his opera box, listening to Tannhäuser in a state of ecstasy, discovering the tragedy of his own soul in the prelude of this grand work of art.</t4614>
<t4615>Once, he developed an interest in jewels.</t4615> <t4616>At a costume ball, he appeared dressed as the French Admiral Anne de Joyeuse, wearing a dress adorned with five hundred and sixty pearls.</t4616> <t4617>This fascination occupied him for years, and it wouldn't be an exaggeration to say it never faded.</t4617> <t4618>Often, he would spend an entire day taking out and putting back the precious stones he had collected: chrysoberyls that turned olive green to red under the lamp light, cymophanes with their silver thread-like lines, pistachio-colored peridots, rose-pink and wine-yellow topazes, fiery red rubies with their four-rayed twinkling stars, flame-red cinnamon stones, orange and purple rubies, and amethysts composed of alternating layers of ruby and sapphire.</t4618> <t4619>Dorian loved the broken rainbows within the star stones and the pearly white of the moonstones.</t4619> <t4620>He had bought three extraordinarily large and beautifully colored emeralds from Amsterdam; he also had a turquoise of the la vieille roche variety that was legendary among gem enthusiasts.</t4620>
<t4621>Dorian was discovering interesting stories about jewels.</t4621><t4622>For example, in Alphonso's "Clericals Disciplina," there was mention of a dragon with eyes made of real purple hyacinth stone.</t4622><t4623>In the exciting life story of Alexander, it was said that the Conqueror of Emathia saw snakes in the Jordan Valley with rings of real emeralds on their backs.</t4623><t4624>Philostratus described that a jewel was hidden in the brains of dragons, and that the monster would fall into a magical sleep when confronted with "golden letters and a red cloak," making it possible to kill it this way.</t4624><t4625>According to the famous alchemist Pierre de Boniface, diamonds had something that made a person invisible, and Indian agate had something that made one speak beautifully.</t4625><t4626>The Solomon stone soothed anger, the hyacinth stone induced sleep, and the amethyst dispelled the fumes of wine.</t4626><t4627>The garnet repelled devils and demons, and the hydropicus dimmed the color of the moon.</t4627><t4628>The selenite brightened and dimmed in accordance with the waxing and waning of the moon.</t4628><t4629>The effect of the meloseus stone, which ensured the capture of thieves, could only be activated by the blood of a boy.</t4629><t4630>Leonardus Camillus had seen a white stone used as an antidote being extracted from the brain of a newly killed frog.</t4630><t4631>The antidote stone found in the hearts of Arabian deer was an amulet that cured the plague.</t4631><t4632>According to Democritus, the aspilat stone found in the nests of birds in Arabia protected people from the dangers of fire.</t4632>
<t4633>The King of Ceylon had ridden through the city on horseback, carrying a large ruby, during his coronation ceremony.</t4633> <t4634>The garden gates of Priest King John’s palace were made of sardius inlaid with the horns of horned serpents so that no one could bring poison inside.</t4634> <t4635>Two golden apples, each containing a garnet, were hung on the eaves of its pointed roof, making the gold shine during the day and the garnet at night.</t4635> <t4636>According to what Lodge wrote in that strange adventure story called “An American Margarita,” it was possible to see “all the untouched ladies of the world” by looking into the magnificent mirrors made of chrysalis with silver carvings, garnet, sapphire, and emerald in the queen's room.</t4636> <t4637>Marco Polo, on the other hand, had seen the natives of Zipangu place rose-colored pearls in the mouths of their deceased.</t4637> <t4638>A sea monster, struck by a pearl at the bottom, killed the thief diver who brought it to King Perozes and mourned the lost pearl for seven months.</t4638> <t4639>Prokopius tells this story: Deceived by the Huns and led to a deep abyss, King Perozes threw the pearl far away, and despite Emperor Anastasius’s promise of five hundred okkas of gold in return, the pearl was never found again.</t4639> <t4640>The King of Malabar also showed a Venetian writer a rosary made of three hundred and four pearls, each representing one of the gods he worshipped.</t4640>
<t4641>VI.</t4641> <t4642>When Alexander's son, the Duke of Valentinois, visited the French King Louis, according to Brantôme, his horse was laden with gold leaf, and the double row of rubies on his headgear radiated an incredible light all around.</t4642> <t4643>The King of England, Charles, had spurs set with four hundred and twenty-one diamonds on his riding horse.</t4643> <t4644>II.</t4644> <t4645>Richard had a jacket valued at three thousand marks, covered with light pink rubies.</t4645> <t4646>King VIII.</t4646> <t4647>Henry, on his way to the Tower of London for his coronation, wore a jacket of embossed gold, a breastplate adorned with diamonds and other precious stones, and a massive "yoke" made of large pink rubies around his neck, as Hall writes.</t4647> <t4648>King I.</t4648> <t4649>James's favorites wore emerald earrings set in filigree gold frames.</t4649> <t4650>II.</t4650> <t4651>Edward gifted Piers Gaveston a suit of armor in red gold inlaid with hyacinth stones, a collar made of golden roses with turquoise centers, and a cap encrusted with pearls.</t4651> <t4652>II.</t4652> <t4653>Henry wore gloves embroidered with jewels up to the elbow; the glove he used for falconry was adorned with twelve rubies and fifty-two large blue pearls.</t4653> <t4654>The last Duke of Burgundy, Charles the Bold, had a ducal hat decorated with pear-shaped pearls and sapphires.</t4654>
<t4655>How splendid life once was!</t4655> <t4656>That pomp, that splendor was so magnificent!</t4656> <t4657>Even reading about the grandeur of the deceased was extraordinary.</t4657>
<t4658>Then Dorian turned his attention to the embroideries, to the tapestries that took the place of wall paintings in the hard-to-heat rooms of Northern European countries.</t4658> <t4659>While examining this subject –he had an extraordinary ability to fully dedicate himself to the subject at hand at the time– he was almost saddened as he thought about the wear and tear of time on beautiful and marvelous things.</t4659> <t4660>Fortunately, at least he had been able to stay away from it.</t4660> <t4661>Summers followed summers, yellow daffodils bloomed and withered countless times, nights filled with horror repeated their shameful stories, but Dorian never changed.</t4661> <t4662>No winter marred his beauty, nor did it wither that fresh bloom.</t4662> <t4663>Yet how different was the fate of tangible things!</t4663> <t4664>Where were they now?</t4664> <t4665>Where was that large, brightly colored shawl, embroidered by dark-skinned girls for the pleasure of Athena, depicting gods fighting giants?</t4665> <t4666>Where was that grand awning that Nero had stretched over the entrance of the Colosseum in Rome; that gigantic purple sail depicting Apollo in his chariot drawn by white horses with golden harnesses and the starry skies?</t4666> <t4667>Dorian longed to see those unique table napkins once embroidered for the Sun Priest: It was known that they depicted all the rare foods and drinks that could be served at feasts.</t4667> <t4668>Then there was King Chilperic's coffin cover embroidered with three hundred golden bees.</t4668> <t4669>And those strange robes that depicted lions, panthers, bears, dogs, forests, rocks, hunters...</t4669> <t4670>in short, everything a painter could copy from nature, which had drawn the ire of the Bishop of Pontus.</t4670> <t4671>And the jacket worn once by King Charles of Orleans, embroidered with the lines of a song that began with "Madame, je suis tout joyeux."</t4671> <t4672>The melody of the song was embroidered on the cuffs of the jacket, with each note, shown in the square style of the day, marked by four pearls.</t4672>
<t4673>Dorian had also read the description of the room in the Palace of Rheims prepared for the Burgundian Queen Joan, adorned with thirteen hundred and twenty-one parrots bearing the king's coat of arms on their wings and five hundred and sixty-one butterflies bearing the queen's coat of arms on their wings – all embroidered with gold thread.</t4673> <t4674>Catherine de Medicis had a mourning bed made for herself from black velvet sprinkled with crescent moon and sun embroideries.</t4674> <t4675>The bed's curtains were made of Damascus fabric with a pattern of wreaths covered with leaves on a gold and silver background, and fringes lined with pearls.</t4675> <t4676>The walls of the room where the bed stood were also adorned with rows of the queen's coats of arms made from cut black velvet on silver fabric.</t4676> <t4677>XIV.</t4677> <t4678>In Louis's room, there were statues of women about seven to eight meters high.</t4678> <t4679>The royal bed of the Polish King Sobieski was made of Izmir silver, with verses from the Quran inlaid with turquoise.</t4679> <t4680>Its legs were silver-gilded, finely carved, and adorned with countless jeweled and enameled medallions.</t4680> <t4681>It had been seized from the Turkish encampment near Vienna and had once housed the banner of Muhammad under its trembling canopy.</t4681>
<t4682>Here it is; for a whole year, Dorian dedicated himself to collecting the finest products that the arts of weaving and embroidery could find.</t4682> <t4683>Delicate Delhi muslins embroidered with gold threads, palm leaf patterns, and iridescent beetle wings; Dhaka muslins, known in the East for their transparency as "woven air," "running water," and "evening dews"; Java fabrics with strange patterns; Chinese curtains with intricate designs; tawny satins or books bound in blonde blue silks, printed with fleur de lys, birds, and human figures; Hungarian needlework veils; Georgian embroideries with gold coins; Japanese Fukusas with greenish gold threads and dazzling feathered birds.</t4683>
<t4684>Dorian had a profound curiosity about the vestments of the church, just as he did about everything related to its ceremonies.</t4684> <t4685>In the west-facing gallery of his house, he had collected many rare and beautiful examples of the attire worn by the Brides of Christ, stored in long cedar chests.</t4685> <t4686>These women, to conceal their pale bodies wasting away due to the sufferings they endured by their own will and the tortures they inflicted upon themselves, were compelled to wear purple silks, expensive linens, and adorn themselves with jewelry.</t4686> <t4687>Dorian also possessed a magnificent priest's robe made of Damascus work woven with gold thread and crimson silk, adorned with golden pomegranates among six-petaled flowers, with pineapples embroidered with seed pearls on either side.</t4687> <t4688>The belts were divided into segments depicting scenes from the life of the Virgin Mary, with her coronation illustrated on the hood.</t4688> <t4689>This was an Italian piece from the fifteenth century.</t4689> <t4690>There was also a robe made of green velvet, embroidered with acanthus leaves clustered in the shape of hearts; the details of the long-stemmed white flowers emerging from these leaves were embroidered with colored beads and gold thread.</t4690> <t4691>On the hood, there was an angel's head filled with gold thread embroidery.</t4691> <t4692>The belts were woven from red and gold silk, speckled with medallions of many saints and martyrs (including St.</t4692> <t4693>Sebastian).</t4693>
<t4694>Dorian also had many Catholic priest robes in his possession.</t4694> <t4695>These robes, made of amber-colored silk, blue silk and brocade with gold embroidery, yellow silk Damascus work with gold thread, were adorned with scenes depicting the Passion and Crucifixion of Jesus, as well as images of lions and peacocks.</t4695> <t4696>At the same time, Dorian had collected white satin bishop's robes decorated with tulip, dolphin, and fleur-de-lys patterns, pink silk Damascus work bishop's robes, red velvet, blue linen altar cloths, towels used in the Holy Communion, chalice covers, and the handkerchiefs said to have wiped Jesus' sweat on his way to the cross.</t4696> <t4697>There was something in the mystical purposes these objects served that stirred his imagination.</t4697> <t4698>Because all these treasures, or rather everything he had gathered in that magnificent house, were for Dorian Gray a means of oblivion: ways to escape, at least for a while, from the fear that sometimes seemed unbearably great to him.</t4698> <t4699>He had hung that terrifying portrait, in which he saw the true downfall of his life in its changing lines, on the wall of that locked, desolate room where he had spent much of his childhood, and had draped the purple funeral cloth embroidered with gold in front of it as a curtain.</t4699> <t4700>There were times when he would forget that disgusting, painted object for weeks by not going up to that room.</t4700> <t4701>Then he would regain the lightness and joy of his heart and devote himself once again to the joy of mere existence.</t4701> <t4702>Then, suddenly, in the middle of the night, he would secretly leave his house and go to the slums around Blue Gate Fields, staying until he was chased away.</t4702> <t4703>When he returned home, he would sit in front of the portrait.</t4703> <t4704>Sometimes he was filled with disgust towards the portrait and himself.</t4704> <t4705>Other times, succumbing to the pride of individuality that constitutes half the allure of sin, he would secretly smile with a sense of hidden satisfaction in front of that grotesque image that actually had to bear the burden he deserved.</t4705>
<t4706>Realizing that he could not bear to be away from England for long periods after a few years, he gave up both the villa he shared with Lord Henry in Trouville and the small white house with high-walled courtyard they visited for several winters in Algeria.</t4706> <t4707>He could not bear to be separated from painting, which was such an inseparable part of his life.</t4707> <t4708>Despite the grille he had installed on the door of the room, he feared that someone might find their way into the room in his absence.</t4708>
<t4709>Dorian knew that anyone entering the room wouldn't understand anything anyway.</t4709> <t4710>The portrait, despite all its hideousness and ugliness, still resembled him, but what would it mean to those who saw it?</t4710> <t4711>Dorian could laugh off those who tried to mock him.</t4711> <t4712>He hadn't painted the portrait himself!</t4712> <t4713>What did it matter to him that it looked so vile and disgraceful?</t4713> <t4714>Even if Dorian told the truth, would anyone believe him?</t4714>
<t4715>He was always afraid.</t4715> <t4716>Sometimes, while he was enjoying himself with the young socialites from his own class at his large house in Nottinghamshire, astonishing this provincial environment with the extreme luxury and dazzling splendor of his lifestyle, he would suddenly leave his guests, rush to the city to make sure that the door to the room had not been tampered with and that the portrait was still in its place.</t4716> <t4717>What if the portrait was stolen?</t4717> <t4718>The very thought of it made Dorian shudder with terror.</t4718> <t4719>That would be when the whole world would learn his secret.</t4719> <t4720>Perhaps those around him were already beginning to sense this secret.</t4720>
<t4721>Because although many were captivated by his charm, there were also those who did not like him.</t4721> <t4722>His membership in a club in the West End district, which he was fully entitled to join due to his family and social status, was almost blocked as a result of a secret ballot.</t4722> <t4723>It was said that once, when a friend brought him to the smoking room of the Churchill Club, the Duke of Berwick and another gentleman stood up and walked out with a meaningful gesture.</t4723> <t4724>After he turned twenty-five, strange rumors about him began to circulate.</t4724> <t4725>It was said that he was seen fighting with foreign sailors in a shabby tavern in a remote corner of the Whitechapel district, that he mingled with thieves and counterfeiters, and that he knew their trade secrets.</t4725> <t4726>He had gained a bad reputation for disappearing without reason.</t4726> <t4727>When he returned to society, many people whispered in corners, smiled contemptuously as they passed by, or scrutinized him with cold, piercing looks as if determined to learn his secret.</t4727>
<t4728>Dorian didn't even care about such insolence and disrespect, of course.</t4728> <t4729>The open-hearted, agile, cheerful attitudes, the friendly, childlike smile, and the endless sweetness of that marvelous youth that never faded from him were, in the eyes of many, the most sufficient response to the slanders against him – because they called these rumors slander.</t4729> <t4730>On the other hand, it was noticeable that some of those who were most intimate with him started to avoid him after a while.</t4730> <t4731>It was observed that women who had fallen madly in love with him, who had faced all the condemnation of society for his sake, and who had defied traditions, turned pale either from shame or horror when he entered the room.</t4731>
<t4732>The scandals whispered from ear to ear only served to increase his strange, dangerous allure in the eyes of many.</t4732><t4733>His great wealth was also a certain source of security for him.</t4733><t4734>Society, at least the society of civilized nations, is never willing to believe anything said against someone who is both rich, attractive, and interesting.</t4734><t4735>It has an instinctive belief that the rules of courtesy are more important than the rules of morality, and in its view, even the greatest respectability is less important than employing a good chef.</t4735><t4736>After all, what good is it to know that someone who sets a poor table and serves inferior wine is impeccable in their private life?</t4736><t4737>As Lord Henry once said on this subject, not even the highest virtues can atone for the sin of the main course arriving cold at the table!</t4737><t4738>We believe his view is very well-founded.</t4738><t4739>Because the fundamental laws of high society and the fundamental laws of art are one and the same, or should be.</t4739><t4740>Form is an absolutely indispensable element for society.</t4740><t4741>This form must possess the dignity and insubstantiality of a ceremony and combine the artificial nature of a romantic play with the wit and beauty that make such plays endearing to us.</t4741><t4742>Is artificiality bad?</t4742><t4743>I don't think so.</t4743><t4744>It is merely a method that allows us to multiply and diversify our personalities.</t4744>
<t4745>More precisely, this was Dorian Gray's view.</t4745> <t4746>He was astonished by the shallowness of those who saw the ego in mankind as something simple, permanent, reliable, and singular.</t4746> <t4747>In his eyes, a human was a being with countless senses, carrying within them mysterious legacies of thoughts and feelings, hiding the marks of the dreadful diseases of the dead in their very flesh, a complex creature that transformed from form to form.</t4747> <t4748>Dorian loved wandering through the bare, cold picture gallery of his country house, looking at the portraits of those whose blood ran in his own veins.</t4748> <t4749>There was Philip Herbert.</t4749> <t4750>In his book Memoirs on the Reigns of Queen Elizabeth and King James, Francis Osborne described him as someone "favored by the Court for his handsome face but who lost his handsomeness quickly."</t4750> <t4751>Perhaps Dorian occasionally lived the life of this young Herbert.</t4751> <t4752>Could a strange, poisonous microbe have passed from body to body and reached his own?</t4752> <t4753>Was it a faint memory of that lost beauty that influenced him to make that mad wish, which changed his life so much, while he was idly standing in Basil Hallward's studio, seemingly for no reason?</t4753> <t4754>There stood Sir Anthony Sherard, in his embroidered red trousers, jeweled <t4776>Beside him hung the portrait of his wife, a pale, thin woman dressed in black.</t4776> <t4777>Her blood also flowed in Dorian's veins.</t4777> <t4778>How interesting all this was!</t4778> <t4779>Then, there was his own mother, with a face resembling Lady Hamilton, her lips moist from wine.</t4779> <t4780>Dorian knew what he had inherited from her.</t4780> <t4781>He had taken her beauty
