<t4786>Those eyes seemed to follow Dorian wherever he went.</t4786>
<t4787>On the other hand, just as a person has ancestors from their lineage, they also have ancestors in the literary world who, in terms of type and disposition, are closer to them than the others, and one could more distinctly perceive their influence.</t4787> <t4788>Sometimes it seemed to Dorian as if the entirety of human history consisted solely of his own biography: with its situations and actions, not as he lived them, but as his imagination created them for him, as he envisioned them in his mind and passions.</t4788> <t4789>It felt as though he had known all those different, formidable individuals who had passed through the world stage; those who made sin so magnificent and evil so mysterious.</t4789> <t4790>It was as if their lives in the past – how, it is unknown – were his own life.</t4790>
<t4791>The hero of that marvelous novel, which had so profoundly affected his life, had also harbored the same strange fantasies.</t4791> <t4792>According to what he recounted in the seventh chapter, he had sat in a garden on the island of Capri, in the guise of Tiberius, reading the scandalous books of Elephantis, with a wreath on his head to protect against lightning strikes, surrounded by dwarfs, peacocks, and the music of a flutist mocking a priest.</t4792> <t4793>As Caligula, he had indulged in debauchery with stable boys in green shirts in the palace's courtyard, and had dined with a jewel-adorned horse from an ivory manger.</t4793> <t4794>As Domitian, he had wandered through a corridor lined with marble mirrors, searching with sunken eyes for the shadow of the dagger that would end his life; sick and fallen with that dreadful taedium vitae, the weariness of life that befalls those to whom life has spared nothing, he had watched the bloody chaos in the arena through a transparent emerald he wore over his eye; and as he rode in a mother-of-pearl and purple-colored chariot drawn by silver-shod mules down Pomegranate Street towards the House of God, he had heard the people shout, "Nero Caesar!"</t4794> <t4795>As Elagabalus, he had painted his face with colorful paints; mingled with women and spun silk; and had brought the Moon from Carthage to marry it mystically with the Sun.</t4795>
<t4796>Dorian would read this extraordinary chapter over and over again; and the two chapters that followed it as well.</t4796> <t4797>On these pages, the terrifying and beautiful images of famous individuals, driven mad and monstrous by evil passions, violence, and weariness, were depicted as if shown in a strange wall tapestry or an enameled decoration: the Duke of Milan, Filippo, who killed his wife and then painted the lips of her corpse with a red poison so that her lover would receive death from the dead; II.</t4797> <t4798>Paul, also known as Formosus due to his pride, a Venetian named Pietro Barbi who obtained his crown, valued at two thousand florins, in exchange for a dreadful sin; Gian Maria Visconti, who hunted humans with hounds, and whose corpse, after being killed, was covered with roses by a prostitute who had loved him; Borgia on his white horse, with Brother's Blood beside him, and Peretto's bloodstain on his cloak; the one whose beauty matched his immorality, who decorated his tent made of white and red silk with water nymphs and centaurs to welcome Leonora of Aragon, and in this feast, dipped a boy in gold to serve as a prize or a substitute for Hylas, VI.</t4798> <t4799>Sixtus's son and favorite, the young Archbishop of Florence, Cardinal Pietro Riario; Ezzelin, who could only escape his melancholy by watching death scenes, who was as addicted to red blood as others were to red wine, rumored to be the devil's son and to have used loaded dice while gambling with his father for his soul; Giambattista Cibo, mockingly called "Innocent," who had the blood of three young men injected into his numbed veins by a Jewish doctor; Sigismondo Malatesta, lover of Isotta and Lord of Rimini, who strangled Polyssena with a handkerchief and poisoned Ginevra d'Esté with a cup of emerald, who, for a shameful passion, founded a church for idol worship, considered an enemy of both man and God in Rome, and whose images were burned; VI.</t4799> <t4800>Charles, who was warned by a leper that he was about to go mad due to his insane love for his brother's wife, and when his mind became diseased and deranged, found solace only in Arabian playing cards illustrated with images of love, death, and madness; Grifonetto Baglioni, who killed Astorre and his wife, Simonetto and his servant, and whose beauty, as he lay dying in the yellow square of Perugia, moved even those who hated him to tears, and who was finally blessed by Atalanta, who had cursed him, with his ornate vest and jeweled cap, and his hair in hyacinth curls.</t4800>
<t4801>There was a spine-chilling allure in all of this.</t4801> <t4802>Dorian saw them at night, and during the day his mind was disturbed by these images.</t4802> <t4803>The Renaissance era had witnessed various, strange methods of poisoning: Helmets, a burning torch, embroidered gloves, jeweled fans, gilded perfume bottles, and amber necklaces were used in poisoning.</t4803> <t4804>But Dorian Gray was poisoned by a book.</t4804> <t4805>Dorian only saw evil as a means to realize the concept of beauty in his mind.</t4805>

<t4827>The twelfth chapter</t4827>
<t4828>Dorian would often recall later that the event took place on November ninth, on the eve of his thirty-eighth birthday.</t4828>
<t4829>On his way back from the dinner he had at Lord Henry's house, he was walking home around eleven o'clock.</t4829> <t4830>He was wrapped in his fur coat, because it was a cold, foggy night.</t4830> <t4831>A man passed by quickly through the fog, near Grosvenor Square and South Audley Street.</t4831> <t4832>He had the collar of his long, gray, loose coat turned up and was carrying a bag in his hand.</t4832> <t4833>Dorian recognized him.</t4833> <t4834>It was Basil Hallward.</t4834> <t4835>A strange fear, the cause of which he could not understand, seized Dorian.</t4835> <t4836>Without showing any sign of recognition, he quickened his steps in the direction of his own house.</t4836>

<t5362>Chapter Fourteen</t5362>

<t5804>Fifteenth chapter</t5804>
<t5805>That evening at half-past eight, dressed extremely elegantly with a large bouquet of Parma violets on his lapel, Dorian Gray was taken to Lady Narborough's drawing room by the servants who bowed respectfully before him.</t5805> <t5806>The veins in his temples throbbed with tension, and he was filled with a fervent excitement.</t5806> <t5807>But as he bowed to kiss the hostess's hand, his demeanor was as relaxed and courteous as ever.</t5807> <t5808>Perhaps when one has to play a role, they appear even more at ease than they are.</t5808> <t5809>Indeed, none of those who looked at Dorian Gray that night would have believed that he had experienced a tragedy so dreadful it could rival any tragedy of our age.</t5809> <t5810>Those well-shaped fingers could not have held a knife with the intent to sin, those smiling lips could not have cursed God and goodness.</t5810> <t5811>Even he was surprised by the calmness of his behavior.</t5811> <t5812>For a moment, he savored the intense pleasure of leading a double life with all its sharpness.</t5812>

<t6136>Chapter Sixteen</t6136>
<t6137>A cold rain had started to fall; the blurry street lamps in the splashing mist resembled dead eyes.</t6137> <t6138>It was exactly the closing time for the taverns; faint silhouettes of men and women were forming scattered groups in front of the doors.</t6138> <t6139>From some of the bars, hair-raising laughter echoed, while in others, drunks were shouting and fighting.</t6139>
<t6140>Dorian, leaning back in the car with his hat pulled down over his eyes, was gazing with a blank stare at the filthy, vile depravity of the big city and occasionally repeating to himself the words he had heard from Lord Henry on the day they first met: "To cure the soul by means of the senses, and the senses by means of the soul..."</t6140> <t6141>Yes, that was the crux of the matter.</t6141> <t6142>Dorian had tried this many times, and now he would try it again.</t6142> <t6143>There were opium dens where "forgetfulness" could be bought with money, dens of horror where you could erase the memories of old sins with the madness of new ones.</t6143>
<t6144>The moon was swaying on the horizon like a yellow skull.</t6144> <t6145>Occasionally, a huge, crooked cloud would stretch out its long arm and hide the moon from view.</t6145> <t6146>Now the street lamps had thinned out, the streets were narrower, more enclosed.</t6146> <t6147>At one point, the coachman lost his way and had to turn back almost half a kilometer.</t6147> <t6148>Steam was rising from the body of the horse as it splashed through puddles.</t6148> <t6149>A mist cut from gray cloth had covered the side windows of the carriage.</t6149>
<t6150>“To heal the soul through the senses, and the senses through the soul!”</t6150> <t6151>How these words echoed in his ears!</t6151> <t6152>Indeed, his soul was mortally ill at this moment.</t6152> <t6153>Was it really possible for the senses to heal the soul?</t6153> <t6154>Innocent blood had been shed.</t6154> <t6155>What could pay the price for this?</t6155> <t6156>No, nothing could pay for this.</t6156> <t6157>However, even if forgiveness was not possible, forgetting was still an option.</t6157> <t6158>Dorian was determined to forget, to erase this memory, to crush and destroy it like one would crush a venomous snake that had bitten them.</t6158> <t6159>Besides, with what right had Basil said those words to him?</t6159> <t6160>Who had appointed him as a judge over others?</t6160> <t6161>He had said things that were terrible, disgusting, unbearable.</t6161>
<t6162>The carriage was still moving, but it seemed to Dorian that it was slowing down with every step.</t6162> <t6163>Dorian lifted the front flap and shouted at the driver to go faster.</t6163> <t6164>The disgusting craving for opium had started to gnaw at him.</t6164> <t6165>His throat was burning, and his slender hands were clenching and unclenching with nerves.</t6165> <t6166>He struck the horse wildly with his cane.</t6166> <t6167>The driver laughed and cracked his whip.</t6167> <t6168>When Dorian laughed in response, the man fell silent.</t6168>
<t6169>The end of the road seemed like it would never come, the roads resembled the pitch-black web of a giant spider.</t6169> <t6170>The monotony of the journey had become unbearable.</t6170> <t6171>As the fog thickened, Dorian began to feel afraid.</t6171>
<t6172>Then they began to pass by desolate brick fields.</t6172> <t6173>Here the mist had lightened; Dorian could make out those strange, bottle-shaped kilns, and the fan-shaped orange flames inside them.</t6173> <t6174>A dog barked after the carriage, and somewhere far off in the darkness, a wandering seagull cried.</t6174> <t6175>The horse stumbled and lurched as it stepped into a pothole in the road; then it reared up.</t6175>
<t6176>After a while, they left the muddy road and clattered through the bumpy cobblestone streets.</t6176> <t6177>Most of the windows around here were dark, yet occasionally, strange shadows were cast on a curtain lit by a lamp, and Dorian watched them with curiosity.</t6177> <t6178>The shadows moved like giant puppets, waving their arms as if they were living creatures.</t6178> <t6179>Dorian hated them.</t6179> <t6180>A dull resentment filled his heart.</t6180> <t6181>As they turned a corner, a woman shouted something at them from the gap of an open door; two men ran after the carriage for about a hundred steps.</t6181> <t6182>The coachman struck them with his whip.</t6182>
<t6183>They say that passion circles a person's thoughts like a ring and brings them back to where they started.</t6183> <t6184>Indeed, Dorian, with his gnawed lips, kept repeating those meaningful words about soul and sense like a disgusting chant.</t6184> <t6185>So much so that in the end, he began to find a complete explanation of his spiritual state in these words and thus justified his passions with the approval of his mind.</t6185> <t6186>But even without this approval, these passions would not withdraw their hands from his being, anyway.</t6186> <t6187>This single thought was seeping from one cell in his brain to another, and the mad desire to live, this most dreadful appetite of mankind, was giving strength to every trembling nerve and fiber like sap.</t6187> <t6188>Once, he hated ugliness because it added reality to everything, but now he found it close to himself for the same reason.</t6188> <t6189>The only reality was ugliness.</t6189> <t6190>Vulgar brawls, disgusting slums, the harsh violence of vagrant lives, the baseness of thieves, dogs, and scoundrels, the products of ugliness, were more vivid and distinct than all the beautiful forms that art entered, and all the imaginary shadows that song sang, because of the tangible intensity of the impressions they created.</t6190> <t6191>These were the things Dorian needed to forget.</t6191> <t6192>In three days, he would be free.</t6192>
<t6193>At the beginning of a dark dirt road, the coachman suddenly pulled the reins and stopped.</t6193> <t6194>Behind the low roofs of the houses here, and the jagged clusters of chimneys, black ship masts were rising.</t6194> <t6195>White rings of mist over the courtyards and spars were like ghostly sails.</t6195>
<t6196>The coachman asked in a muffled voice from behind the cover, "Somewhere around here, right, sir?"</t6196>
<t6197>Dorian shuddered and looked around.</t6197> <t6198>“This is good,” he said, quickly getting out, paid the promised fare to the driver, and then walked briskly towards the pier.</t6198> <t6199>Here and there, a lantern on the stern of a large cargo ship was shining.</t6199> <t6200>As the light flickered, the puddles were breaking into a thousand pieces.</t6200> <t6201>A crimson light was cast from a steamship taking on coal in preparation for setting sail.</t6201> <t6202>The slippery pavement resembled a wet raincoat.</t6202>
<t6203>Dorian turned left with quick steps.</t6203> <t6204>Occasionally, he looked back to see if anyone was following him.</t6204> <t6205>In seven or eight minutes, he reached a small, poor-looking house squeezed between two large factories.</t6205> <t6206>There was a lamp in one of the upper floor windows of the house.</t6206> <t6207>Dorian stopped and knocked on the door with a special password.</t6207>
<t6208>After a while, he heard footsteps walking in the corridor and the chain being unhooked.</t6208> <t6209>The door opened quietly.</t6209> <t6210>Dorian entered without saying a word to the short, crooked shadow that shrank back into the shadows as he passed.</t6210> <t6211>The green curtain at the end of the corridor was fluttering and shaking with the strong gust of wind that had followed Dorian inside.</t6211> <t6212>Dorian pushed the curtain aside and entered a low-ceilinged, long room that seemed to have once been used as a third-class dance hall.</t6212> <t6213>The light from the gas lamps flickering loudly on the walls was hitting the fly-specked mirrors in front of them, dimming and distorting.</t6213> <t6214>The oily, corrugated tin reflectors behind the lamps were forming circles of flickering light.</t6214> <t6215>The floors were covered with yellow sawdust, trampled and muddied in places, stained black in rings by spilled drinks.</t6215> <t6216>A few Malays squatting by a small coal stove were playing with bone chips, their white teeth showing as they chatted.</t6216> <t6217>In one corner, a sailor had collapsed onto a table, his head resting on his arms.</t6217> <t6218>In front of the dew-painted bar that covered an entire wall, two shabby, skinny women were mocking an old man.</t6218> <t6219>The man was shaking the cuffs of his jacket with an expression of disgust on his face.</t6219> <t6220>As Dorian passed by, one of the women laughed and said, "He thinks there are red ants on him."</t6220> <t6221>The poor man looked at her in horror, sighed, and sobbed.</t6221>
<t6222>A low staircase at the back of the room led to a dark compartment.</t6222> <t6223>As Dorian ran up the shaky three steps of the staircase, the heavy scent of opium greeted him.</t6223> <t6224>Dorian took a deep breath, his nostrils quivering with pleasure.</t6224> <t6225>When he entered, a young man with straight, blond hair, who was leaning over a lamp lighting a long, thin pipe in his hand, looked up at him and then greeted him with a bit of hesitation.</t6225>
<t6226>Dorian muttered, "So, you're here, Adrian?"</t6226>
<t6227>The other replied in a calm voice, "Where else would I be?"</t6227> <t6228>"None of our children talk to me anymore.</t6228><t6229>"</t6229>
<t6230>“I thought you had left England.</t6230><t6231>”</t6231>
<t6232>“Darlington is not going to do anything.</t6232> <t6233>My brother finally paid the bill.</t6233> <t6234>George is not talking to me either...”</t6234> <t6235>Then the young man sighed and added, “I don't care,”</t6235> <t6236>“Who needs a friend when you have this at your disposal!</t6236> <t6237>I had too many friends anyway, I guess.</t6237><t6238>”</t6238>
<t6239>Dorian winced and grimaced; he looked around at the misshapen things sprawled in impossible positions on the tattered mattresses.</t6239> <t6240>Twisted legs, gaping mouths, lifeless eyes staring blankly seemed to mesmerize him.</t6240> <t6241>He knew in which strange heavens they suffered, and the secrets of the brand-new pleasures they learned in which lightless hells.</t6241> <t6242>Their situation was better than his own.</t6242> <t6243>Because he was in the prison of his thoughts.</t6243> <t6244>Memories were gnawing at his soul like a terrible disease.</t6244> <t6245>He kept seeing the eyes of Basil Hallward looking at him.</t6245> <t6246>Yet he didn't think he could stay here.</t6246> <t6247>The presence of Adrian Singleton disturbed him.</t6247> <t6248>He wanted to be somewhere no one would recognize him.</t6248> <t6249>He wanted to escape from himself.</t6249>
<t6250>After a pause, he said, "I am going to the other place."</t6250>
<t6251>"The one on the dock?</t6251><t6252>"</t6252>
<t6253>“Yes.</t6253><t6254>”</t6254>

<t6258>Dorian shrugged his shoulders.</t6258> <t6259>“I'm tired of women who fall in love with me.</t6259> <t6260>Women who bear a grudge are more interesting.</t6260> <t6261>Besides, the goods there are better.</t6261><t6262>”</t6262>
<t6263>"Hit one with the other."</t6263><t6264></t6264>
<t6265>"I like the one over there more."</t6265> <t6266>"Come and have a drink."</t6266> <t6267>"I need to drink."</t6267><t6268>"</t6268>
<t6269>The young man muttered, "I don't want anything."</t6269>
<t6270>"Don't worry.</t6270><t6271>"</t6271>
<t6272>Adrian Singleton, with a weary demeanor, got up and followed Dorian to the bar.</t6272> <t6273>A half-breed with a shabby turban and a worn-out coat greeted them with a dirty grin and placed a bottle of brandy and two glasses in front of them.</t6273> <t6274>The women approached and started to chatter.</t6274> <t6275>Dorian turned his back to them and said something in a low voice to Adrian Singleton.</t6275>
<t6276>One of the women, with a mocking smile curving on her face like Malayan daggers, sneered, "Tonight, nothing but our arrogance is visible."</t6276>
<t6277>Dorian stomped his foot and shouted, "For God's sake, don't talk to me!"</t6277> <t6278>"What do you want?</t6278> <t6279>Money? Take it.</t6279> <t6280>Don't ever speak to me again.</t6280><t6281>"</t6281>

<t6285>Adrian sighed, "It's pointless."</t6285> <t6286>"I don't want to go back.</t6286> <t6287>What difference does it make?</t6287> <t6288>I'm perfectly happy here.</t6288><t6289>"</t6289>

<t6291>“Maybe.</t6291><t6292>”</t6292>

<t6296>Dorian walked towards the door with an expression on his face that showed he was in pain.</t6296> <t6297>As he drew the curtain aside, an ugly laugh burst from the painted lips of the woman who had just taken his money.</t6297> <t6298>In a raspy voice, as if caught by a sob, the woman said, "There goes the man bought by the devil!"</t6298>
<t6299>Dorian said, "Damn you."</t6299> <t6300>"Don't say that."</t6300><t6301>"</t6301>
<t6302>The woman snapped her fingers.</t6302> <t6303>“You like the name Sweet Prince, don't you?” she shouted after him.</t6303>
<t6304>Upon hearing these words, the sailor who was dozing at the table jumped up and scanned his surroundings with wild eyes.</t6304> <t6305>He heard the sound of a door closing.</t6305> <t6306>The man ran outside as if he were chasing someone.</t6306>
<t6307>Dorian Gray was walking briskly along the quay under the drizzle.</t6307><t6308>Meeting Adrian Singleton had affected him in a strangely emotional way: Was the blame for the ruin of this fresh life really to be placed on his shoulders, as Basil Hallward had so basely suggested?</t6308><t6309>Dorian bit his lip, his eyes filled with sorrow for a moment.</t6309><t6310>On the other hand, what did this matter have to do with him?</t6310><t6311>Human life was too short to bear the weight of others' mistakes.</t6311><t6312>Everyone lived their own life and paid the price for living it.</t6312><t6313>The tragic part was that a person had to pay for a single mistake many times over.</t6313><t6314>They had to keep paying, that was the truth of it.</t6314><t6315>In its dealings with humans, Fate never closed its ledger.</t6315><t6316>According to psychologists, there are moments in life when the passion for sin—or for what society deems sinful—takes such hold of a person's nature that every fiber of the body, every cell of the brain, seems to revolt with dreadful impulses.</t6316><t6317>At such times, men and women lose the independence of their will.</t6317><t6318>They move towards their impending fate like programmed machines.</t6318><t6319>The ability to choose has been taken from them.</t6319><t6320>Conscience has been killed, and if it exists at all, it lives only to add allure to rebellion and to make stubbornness seem charming.</t6320><t6321>For the source of all sin, as theologians tirelessly say, is great disobedience.</t6321><t6322>If that exalted spirit, the morning star of sin, was cast out of heaven, it was because he was rebellious.</t6322>
<t6323>With his heart hardened from being mired in sinful thoughts, his mind darkened, and his soul hungry for rebellion, Dorian Gray continued to walk at full speed, quickening his steps more and more.</t6323> <t6324>Then, as he turned into a dim archway, as he often did to take a shortcut to the bad place he was heading, he felt someone suddenly grab him from behind.</t6324> <t6325>Before he had a chance to defend himself, he was pushed against the wall, a ruthless hand gripping his throat.</t6325>
<t6326>Dorian began to struggle frantically to save his life, pulling away the fingers that were squeezing his throat with tremendous effort.</t6326> <t6327>In an instant, he heard the click of a gun, saw the glint of a polished barrel aimed at his head, and made out the silhouette of a short, stocky man standing before him.</t6327>
<t6328>"What do you want?" he asked haltingly.</t6328>
<t6329>"Shut up," said the man.</t6329> <t6330>"If you move, I'll shoot.</t6330><t6331>"</t6331>

<t6438>The seventeenth chapter</t6438>
<t6439>A week had passed.</t6439> <t6440>Dorian Gray was sitting in the conservatory of Royal Selby, talking with the beautiful Duchess of Monmouth.</t6440> <t6441>The duchess's husband, a weary-looking man in his sixties, was also among the guests.</t6441> <t6442>It was tea time.</t6442> <t6443>The soft light of the large lamp with a lace shade on the table illuminated the fine porcelain and wrought silver of the tea set.</t6443> <t6444>The duchess was serving.</t6444> <t6445>As her white hands gently moved among the cups, her full red lips were smiling at something Dorian had whispered.</t6445> <t6446>Lord Henry, who was reclining in a wicker chair covered with silk, was observing them.</t6446> <t6447>Lady Narborough, who was sitting on a peach-colored sofa, was pretending to listen to the duke describing the latest Brazilian insect he had added to his collection.</t6447> <t6448>Three young men, dressed in stylish, pretentious tuxedos, were distributing cakes to the ladies.</t6448> <t6449>Twelve people had gathered at the mansion that day.</t6449> <t6450>More guests were expected to arrive the next day.</t6450>

<t7507>Twentieth chapter</t7507>

<t7626>Now, about that murder...</t7626> <t7627>Would it cling to him for life?</t7627> <t7628>Would Dorian carry the burden of his past for life?</t7628> <t7629>Would he really confess his crime?</t7629> <t7630>Never.</t7630> <t7631>There was only one piece of evidence left against him: the portrait itself; the only evidence.</t7631> <t7632>Dorian would get rid of it.</t7632> <t7633>Why had he kept it for so long anyway?</t7633> <t7634>Once, he used to take pleasure in watching the picture change and age.</t7634> <t7635>Lately, even that pleasure was gone.</t7635> <t7636>He couldn't sleep at night because of this picture.</t7636> <t7637>When he was away from home, he was terrified that other eyes would see the picture.</t7637> <t7638>His fiery emotions were overshadowed by sadness because of this picture.</t7638> <t7639>The mere memory of this picture had spoiled many pleasant moments.</t7639> <t7640>It was as if the portrait had become his conscience.</t7640> <t7641>Yes, it had become his conscience.</t7641> <t7642>Dorian had to get rid of the portrait.</t7642>
<t7643>When he looked around, he saw the knife that killed Basil Hallward.</t7643> <t7644>How many times had he cleaned this knife, leaving no stain on it.</t7644> <t7645>The knife was sparkling, it was shining.</t7645> <t7646>Just as he had killed the painter, he would kill the painter's work and all its meaning.</t7646> <t7647>He would take the life of the past; when the past was dead, Dorian would be free.</t7647> <t7648>The knife would end the life of that disgusting, terrifying soul; when the portrait's warnings ceased, Dorian would find peace.</t7648> <t7649>He took the knife and stabbed the painting.</t7649>
<t7650>A shout was heard, a commotion.</t7650> <t7651>The shout was so filled with terrible agony that the servants woke up in fright, and quietly came out of their rooms.</t7651> <t7652>Two gentlemen passing through the square below looked up at the large house.</t7652> <t7653>They went ahead and found a policeman, and brought him back.</t7653> <t7654>No matter how many times the policeman rang the doorbell, there was no response.</t7654> <t7655>Except for the light in one of the upper floor windows, the whole house was dark.</t7655> <t7656>After a while, the policeman walked away, went up to the porch of one of the neighboring houses, and started observing the house from there.</t7656>
<t7657>The older of the two gentlemen asked, "Whose house is this, officer?"</t7657>
<t7658>Adam said, "Mr. Dorian Gray, sir."</t7658>
<t7659>The gentlemen exchanged glances, curling their lips in disdain as they walked away.</t7659> <t7660>One of them was a relative of Sir Henry Ashton.</t7660>
<t7661>Inside, in the servants' quarters of the house, half-naked maids were whispering to each other.</t7661> <t7662>Old Ms. Leaf was crying, rubbing her hands together.</t7662> <t7663>Francis was as pale as a corpse.</t7663>
<t7664>Fifteen minutes later, they took the coachman and one of the male servants and quietly went upstairs.</t7664> <t7665>They knocked on the door but received no answer.</t7665> <t7666>They called out.</t7666> <t7667>There was neither a sound nor a breath.</t7667> <t7668>After trying in vain to open the door, they finally climbed up to the roof and jumped onto the balcony from there.</t7668> <t7669>The glass door of the balcony opened easily; its bolt was worn out.</t7669>
<t7670>When they entered, they found a magnificent portrait of their master on the wall: Just as they had last seen him, dazzling with his unique youth and beauty, breathtaking.</t7670> <t7671>On the floor lay a man in a tuxedo, with a knife plunged into his heart.</t7671> <t7672>His face was wrinkled, sunken, extremely repulsive.</t7672> <t7673>It was only when they examined his rings that they could identify who he was.</t7673>
